        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adma.202418219')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="84466fed8cc84d99956bf9ff4bb5d269">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '1fc30212-9eda-4dc2-bfef-efc09e249563'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d13056331" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Avoiding severe structural distortion, irreversible phase transition, and realizing the stabilized multielectron redox are vital for promoting the development of high-performance NASICON-type cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Herein, a high-entropy Na<sub>3.45</sub>V<sub>0.4</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Ti<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.45</sub>Cr<sub>0.35</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP) cathode material is prepared by ultrafast high-temperature shock, which inhibits the possibility of phase separation and achieves reversible and stable multielectron transfer of 2.4/2.8 e<sup>−</sup> at voltage range of 2.0–4.45/1.5–4.45 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na (the capacity of 137.2/162.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). The galvanostatic charge/discharge and in-situ X-ray diffraction tests indicate the sequential redox reactions and approximate solid solution phase transition behavior of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP. Density functional theory calculations analyze the migration pathways and energy barriers, further confirming the superior reaction kinetics of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP. Accordingly, the HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP exhibits outstanding wide temperature applicability and can operate stably in the temperature range of −50–60&nbsp;°C, accompanied by a capacity retention of 92.8% after 400 cycles at −40&nbsp;°C and a capacity of 73.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at −50&nbsp;°C. The assembled hard carbon//HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP full-cell offers an energy density of ≈301 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> based on total cathode and anode active mass, verifying the application feasibility of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP. This work provides an innovative and ultrafast pathway to rationally fabricate high-performance cathodes for SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202418219-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202418219-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The burgeoning paradigm of rechargeable lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) is constrained by the scarcity and uneven nature of lithium resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0001_R_d13056323e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Chem. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this context, due to the similar working principles, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with abundant sodium resources have great application potential in the large-scale energy storage system.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0002_R_d13056323e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Sci. Adv. Energy. Mater. Rare Met. Green. Energy. Environ. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the development of high-performance SIBs requires the integration of multiple peculiarities, such as high capacity, good cycling stability, and rapid reaction kinetics, which are largely determined by the cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0002_R_d13056323e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Sci. Adv. Energy. Mater. Rare Met. Green. Energy. Environ. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among the cathode materials of SIBs, Na super ionic conductor (NASICON)-type polyanionic compounds are one of the brightest candidates by the merit of 3D open framework structure, compositional and structural diversity, as well as exceptional Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0003_R_d13056323e556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater. Carbon Neutralization Int. J. Miner. Metall. Mater. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. Chem. Eng. J.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a typical NASICON cathode, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) can deliver a reversible specific capacity of 117.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> based on the redox reaction of V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup> at 3.4 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, corresponding to the transfer of two electrons.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0004_R_d13056323e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the lower energy density compared to the commercial LIBs cathode materials and vanadium intrinsic drawbacks of toxicity and high price hinder the practical application of NVP in SIBs. Therefore, it is highly desirable to exploit cost-effective low-vanadium even vanadium-free NASICON cathode materials with multielectron reaction and high redox potential.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0005_R_d13056323e584" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy. Environ. Mater. Acta Phys.-Chim. Sin.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>At present, tremendous efforts have been devoted to increase the electron transfer and regulate the mean operating voltage by introducing diverse active transition metal (TM) redox centers into NASICON framework.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0006_R_d13056323e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces. Adv. Mater. Sci. China. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, several binary and ternary NASICON cathode materials have been explored in an attempt to break the energy density bottleneck of phosphates.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0007_R_d13056323e596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Energy. Lett. Chin. Chem. Lett.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, in the multielectron redox reaction process, there are often associated with a series of obstacles, including the capacity irreversibility, the crystal structure degradation and significant volume change, which lead to the inferior cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0008_R_d13056323e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Small Methods Chem. Mater. InfoMat">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As an emerging and feasible mean, high-entropy strategy has been verified to stabilize the crystal structure during de-/sodiation, which features the multi-principal elements sharing a single crystallographic site and co-existing in a single phase with equimolar or near-equimolar.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0002_R_d13056323e607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Sci. Adv. Energy. Mater. Rare Met. Green. Energy. Environ. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy. Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0009_R_d13056323e607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Energy. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high configurational entropy enhances the solid solubility of the different metals in the host lattice, which in turn supports the formation of solid-solution phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0009_R_d13056323e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Energy. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, in resent two years, high-entropy strategy has been applied successfully in various cathode materials of SIBs, such as layered oxides, transition metal hexacyanoferrate and polyanionic compounds, also demonstrating the unique properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0010_R_d13056323e618" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Inorg. Chem.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, employing the high-entropy strategy to design cathode materials is still a complex and arduous task as it exists inevitably lattice distortion due to different ionic size and bond energy between elements, which leads to the high difficulty in the formation of solid-solution phase high-entropy NASICON cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0002_R_d13056323e623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Sci. Adv. Energy. Mater. Rare Met. Green. Energy. Environ. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Generally, the preparation of polyanionic phosphate materials is mainly based on the heat treatment in tube furnaces, which requires a long time of heating, holding, and cooling (often 12 h or more). This synthesis process is adverse to the formation of single phase high-entropy NASICON materials, and it is difficult to achieve rapid, efficient, and stable preparation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0011_R_d13056323e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To overcome the aforementioned issues, herein, we present an ultrafast synthesis approach by employing high temperature shock (HTS) technique for high-entropy NASICON cathode materials Na<sub>3.45</sub>V<sub>0.4</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Ti<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.45</sub>Cr<sub>0.35</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP). By introducing high temperature and ultrafast heating/cooling rate (≈10<sup>5</sup>/10<sup>4&nbsp;</sup>°C s<sup>−1</sup>), the single phase HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode was prepared in controllable and efficient manner, which inhibits the possibility of phase separation, and achieves the reversible and stable multielectron transfer of 2.4/2.8 e<sup>−</sup> at voltage range of 2.0–4.45/1.5–4.45 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na (corresponding to the capacity of 137.2/162.0 (0.2C) mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and superior cycling durability (83.7% capacity retention after 2000 cycles at 5C). Moreover, the prepared HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP also demonstrates wide temperature applicability and can work under a wide temperature range of −50–60&nbsp;°C. In situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) and ex-situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) were employed to research the reversible structure evolution and the charge compensation effect of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP during the charging and discharging process, indicating the approaching solid solution phase transition behavior and sequential redox process. Density functional theory (DFT) calculations demonstrate that HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP has ideal migration pathways and energy barriers, which further confirms the superior reaction kinetics. The ultrafast sintering strategy of high-entropy NASICON cathode provides a broad platform for the development of polyanionic materials for long-lifespan and all-climate SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202418219-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202418219-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>In the first place, the five adjacent elements Ti, V, Cr, Mn, and Fe are employed to prepare the near-equimolar NASICON cathode. In order to increase the proportion of cheap and active Mn center, the stoichiometric ratio of Cr is reduced to 0.35 on the equimolar basis and the single-phase high entropy cathode material Na<sub>3.45</sub>V<sub>0.4</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>Ti<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.45</sub>Cr<sub>0.35</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP) was prepared by simple sol-gel assisted HTS technology (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the condition for ultrafast calcination was 800&nbsp;°C for 20 s (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202418219-fig-0001">1a</a>), which not only achieves the effective and controllable preparation of single phase HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP material within a few seconds but also enables the formation of smaller particles and inhibits the possibility of phase separation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0012_R_d13056323e716" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Different from the single transition metal (TM) redox centered NVP, multiple TMs (Ti, V, Cr, Mn, Fe) occupy the same Wyckoff position in HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP by the charge balance mechanism to form a stable single-phase crystal structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0001">1b</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0010_R_d13056323e727" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Inorg. Chem.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The corresponding configuration entropy increases to 1.61R from 0 R of single TM materials, which may create the more possible interaction combination and unique energy storage properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0002_R_d13056323e732" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Sci. Adv. Energy. Mater. Rare Met. Green. Energy. Environ. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0001">1c</a>, the Rietveld refinement analyses of XRD illustrates that all the observed diffraction peaks of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP correspond well to the rhombohedral crystal with a typical trigonal (<i>R-3c</i>) structure. Five TMs dominate the octahedral positions of <i>12c</i> with corresponding molarity, and the lantern-like 3D open framework in crystal structure furnishes beneficial channels for Na<sup>+</sup> migration (the inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0001">1c</a>). The detailed structural information on lattice parameters, atomic coordinates, and occupancy are given in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). Moreover, the stretching vibrations of TM─O bonds in octahedra and P─O bonds in tetrahedra were recognized by Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) result (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a>, Supporting Information). Raman spectrum in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2b</a> (Supporting Information) shows two discernable carbon peaks at ≈1345 cm<sup>−1</sup> (D-band) and 1593 cm<sup>−1</sup> (G-band). The intensity ratio (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) is slightly less than 1, the partial graphitization of the carbon is benefit to the electronic conductivity of materials. To define the valence state of TMs, the HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP was preliminarily detected by XPS (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2c</a> (Supporting Information) and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0001">1d</a>). Survey spectrum clearly demonstrates the existence of Na, Mn, V, Fe, Cr, Ti, P, O, and C. The characteristic peaks located at 641.81 eV (Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub>) and 653.56 eV (Mn 2p<sub>1/2</sub>) can be indexed to the Mn<sup>2+</sup>, and the peaks at 516.86 and 523.27 eV corresponds to V<sup>3+</sup>. For the Fe 2p spectrum, the peaks at 711.48 and 724.42 eV belong to Fe<sup>2+</sup>, and the peaks at 714.95 and 726.65 eV are the characteristic peaks of Fe<sup>3+</sup>. The high resolution XPS spectrum of Cr 2p can be deconvoluted into two main peaks at 577.15 and 587.1 eV, which can be ascribed to Cr<sup>3+</sup>. It is worth noting that Ti also shows mixed states. Specifically, the peaks centered at 458.57 and 464.28 eV imply Ti<sup>3+</sup>, and peaks located at 459.59 and 465.64 eV can be indexed to the Ti<sup>4+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0002_R_d13056323e801" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Sci. Adv. Energy. Mater. Rare Met. Green. Energy. Environ. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202418219-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ad6620db-71f7-4269-9548-8201ec7d2e2c/adma202418219-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ad6620db-71f7-4269-9548-8201ec7d2e2c/adma202418219-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c6617025-f24e-4e25-9881-ede53d1b9cdd/adma202418219-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ad6620db-71f7-4269-9548-8201ec7d2e2c/adma202418219-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202418219-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202418219" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The ultrafast calcination process, structure, and morphology characterization of the HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode material. The schematic illustration of a) the ultrafast calcination process and b) the TMs distribution in single TM-based and HE-based NASICON materials. c) XRD Rietveld refinement profile (inset: the crystal structure). d) High-resolution XPS spectrums of Mn 2p, V 2p, Fe 2p, Cr 2p, and Ti 2p. e) SEM image with particle size analysis. f,g) HR-TEM image and TEM-EDS mappings.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscope (TEM) accompanied by energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) mappings were utilized to reveal the particle morphology, interplanar spacing, and elemental distribution. As shown in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0001">1e</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information), HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP martials are polyhedral shape with nanometer size and an average particle size is ≈266 nm, and the carbon nanotubes are evenly distributed between HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP particles. The HR-TEM image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0001">1f</a>) displays a continuous carbon layer of ≈7 nm, which can be linked to the carbon nanotubes, enabling rapid electronic migration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0013_R_d13056323e847" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Well-defined lattice fringes with <i>d</i>-spacings of 0.440 nm points to (104) plane of typical NASICON structure. TEM-based EDS mappings simultaneously demonstrate the uniform distribution of each element (Na, Fe, V, Cr, Ti, Mn, P, O, and C) in the whole particle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0001">1g</a>). Furthermore, the thermogravimetric (TG) analysis indicates the carbon amount is ≈10.2% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a</a>, Supporting Information). The HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP displays the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area of 97.09 m<sup>2</sup>g<sup>−1</sup> and the mesoporous distribution of ≈9.1 nm, which promotes electrolyte penetration (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5b</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0003_R_d13056323e870" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater. Carbon Neutralization Int. J. Miner. Metall. Mater. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. Chem. Eng. J.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performance of the HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode within 2.0–4.45 V was evaluated by galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) and cyclic voltammetry (CV). As shown in <b>Figures</b> <a href="#adma202418219-fig-0002">2a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information), the HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode presents a reversible discharge specific capacity of 137.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1C (1C&nbsp;=&nbsp;150 mA g<sup>−1</sup>) and high energy density of 417 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. The GCD profiles show the continuous and smooth multistep plateaus, indicating HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode undergoes sequential redox reactions. According to the voltage region, the GCD curves can be divided into high voltage (H), medium voltage (M) and low voltage region (L), and the corresponding discharge capacities are 22.7, 55.7, and 58.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The redox reaction results of TMs shown in GCD, dQ/dV and CV curves are consistent (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0002">2b,c</a>), and the voltage plateau in the H-region is 4.0 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, which may be attributes to Mn<sup>3+/4+</sup> and V<sup>4+/5+</sup> redox. The main capacity contribution of the M-region might derive from Mn<sup>2+/3+</sup>, Fe<sup>3+/4+</sup>, and V<sup>3+/4+</sup> redox, and the voltage plateau of the L-region probably correspond to Fe<sup>2+/3+</sup> and Ti<sup>3+/4+</sup> redox. It is noteworthy that there is a tiny peak at ≈3.2 V, which could be attributed to the redox of Fe<sup>3+/4+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0015_R_d13056323e924" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The obvious high voltage plateau demonstrates that the redoxes of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP were successfully activated in H-region. The first three CV curves show three big pairs of redox peaks at 2.2, 3.4, and 4.1 V respectively, which is consistent with GCD and dQ/dV curves. The CV curves of the first three cycles are slightly inconsistent, which may be related to the instability of electrolyte under high voltage. And the fourth and fifth cycle are highly coincident, indicating the appropriate reversibility of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0002">2d</a>, the HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode shows the discharge specific capacity of 119.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5C, and even at high rate of 30 C, the HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP still can deliver the discharge specific capacity of 62.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP also exhibits exceptional cycling stability, retaining the specific capacity of 89.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 1C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, at high rate of 5C, it has the capacity retention of 90.0% after 1000 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0002">2e</a>), and achieves the outstanding capacity retention of 86.7% and 83.7% when the cycle number up to 1500 and 2000. Such satisfactory cyclic stability may be attributed to the entropy-driven stabilization produced by the mixing of the five cations, and the pinning effect introduced by the multiple TMs during the sequential redox reactions to stabilize the crystal structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0005_R_d13056323e956" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy. Environ. Mater. Acta Phys.-Chim. Sin.">5</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0016_R_d13056323e956" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Small">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202418219-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/79b919c9-5211-4b09-af4c-1f7f42e32e25/adma202418219-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/79b919c9-5211-4b09-af4c-1f7f42e32e25/adma202418219-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8874e4be-a5ad-497c-8455-4cd94566db00/adma202418219-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/79b919c9-5211-4b09-af4c-1f7f42e32e25/adma202418219-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202418219-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202418219" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performances of the HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode material in 2.0–4.45 V at 25&nbsp;°C. a) Typical GCD profiles at 0.1C. b) The corresponding dQ/dV curves. c) The first five CV curves at the scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. d) Rate performance and the corresponding GCD curves from 0.1C to 30C. e) Long-term cycling performance at 5C. f) GITT curves and the corresponding diffusion coefficient (inset: the <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> calculated from CV test). g) Comparison of the electrochemical performance for the HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP with other reported materials.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0004" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0004_R_d13056323e990" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy. Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0008" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0008_R_d13056323e990" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Small Methods Chem. Mater. InfoMat">8</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0013" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0013_R_d13056323e990" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy. Mater.">13</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0014" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0014_R_d13056323e990" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano Energy Energy. Storage. Mater. Adv. Energy. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To evaluate the kinetics performance, the CV test at various scan rates and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) were performed. As shown in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0002">2f</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information), the total Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub>) of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP display small fluctuation within the relatively narrow range of 10<sup>−10</sup>–10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, and the diffusion coefficient curves is steady and has no obvious twist. Moreover, according to the results of GITT, the diffusion coefficient can be basically maintained at 10<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> in the discharge stage, indicating the superior Na<sup>+</sup> intercalating kinetics. Compared with&nbsp;other NASICON-type cathodes, this diffusion coefficient is extremely competitive.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0002_R_d13056323e1032" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Sci. Adv. Energy. Mater. Rare Met. Green. Energy. Environ. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy. Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0003_R_d13056323e1032" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater. Carbon Neutralization Int. J. Miner. Metall. Mater. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. Chem. Eng. J.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information), among the currently reported high-entropy NASICON-type polyanionic cathode material,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0002_R_d13056323e1041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Sci. Adv. Energy. Mater. Rare Met. Green. Energy. Environ. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy. Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0004_R_d13056323e1041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy. Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0009_R_d13056323e1041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Energy. Mater.">9</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0010_R_d13056323e1041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Inorg. Chem.">10</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0013_R_d13056323e1041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy. Mater.">13</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0017_R_d13056323e1041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Nat. Energy. J. Electroanal. Chem. Chem. Eng. J.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP material has the highest Na<sup>+</sup> content, which can ensure the supply of Na<sup>+</sup> during the charging/discharging processes and thus stabilize the crystal structure, achieving the high Na-storage capacity and excellent cycling stability. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0002">2g</a> shows that HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP has preponderant electrochemical performance (such as discharge specific capacity, capacity retention, and energy density) and still has a quite modest volume change under the premise of 2.4 electrons reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0004_R_d13056323e1058" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy. Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0008_R_d13056323e1058" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Small Methods Chem. Mater. InfoMat">8</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0013_R_d13056323e1058" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy. Mater.">13</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0014_R_d13056323e1058" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano Energy Energy. Storage. Mater. Adv. Energy. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Further, the in-situ XRD test was employed to monitor the crystal structure evolution of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP during the Na<sup>+</sup> de-/intercalation process. The full-scale 3D color mode in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information) shows the result of the first two cycles collected at 0.2 C without interval, and the typical 2D contour plot demonstrates the evolution of the major diffraction peaks during the second cycle (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202418219-fig-0003">3a</a>). The strong diffraction peaks, such as (104), (113), (024), (211), (116), and (300), are identified, and those diffraction peaks point to the <i>R</i>-3<i>c</i> structure. During the GCD process, all the diffraction peaks undergo the process of shifting toward higher angles and then monotonically returning the low angle, and can fully return to the original Bragg positions and intensities, indicating that the HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP has excellent crystal structural stability and reversibility. Moreover, all diffraction peaks are shift only and without significantly interruption, manifesting the mild approaching solid solution phase transition behavior. Specifically, different from the bi-phasic reaction of mono (NVP), binary (Na<sub>3.4</sub>V<sub>1.6</sub>Fe<sub>0.4</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>) and ternary (Na<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>0.33</sub>V<sub>0.67</sub>Ti(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>) NASICON-type cathode materials,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0014_R_d13056323e1107" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano Energy Energy. Storage. Mater. Adv. Energy. Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0017_R_d13056323e1107" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Nat. Energy. J. Electroanal. Chem. Chem. Eng. J.">17</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0018_R_d13056323e1107" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP has a sequential redox process approaching to solid solution reaction under the action of high configurational entropy, which further elucidates the internal reasons for the excellent electrochemical and kinetic properties. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0003">3b</a>, the lattice parameters are calculated by the refinement of XRD during the first two cycles. During the charging process, the lattice parameter <i>a</i> decreases significantly and <i>c</i> increases slightly, and the discharging process is the opposite, this indicates that the lattice parameters undergo reversible changes. In addition, the slight change of <i>c</i> further make sure the stability of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP. Notably, the HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP experiences a small volume change of ≈6.8%, which is much smaller than most of the NASICON-type cathodes, and the detailed data are classified and compared according to two and more than two electron reactions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0003">3c</a>). Based on the XRD and GCD results, the reaction mechanism of the multielectron redox reaction process in HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP is illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0003">3d</a>. The HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode was extracted about two Na<sup>+</sup> in the first charging stage, followed by the reversible de-/intercalation of ≈2.4 Na<sup>+</sup> in the voltage range of 2.0–4.45 V. All the required Na<sup>+</sup> during the first charging process can be completely supplied by the Na2 site of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP, and the remaining Na in Na1 site could stabilize the structural framework.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0002_R_d13056323e1148" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Sci. Adv. Energy. Mater. Rare Met. Green. Energy. Environ. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202418219-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/210ae635-9f42-4910-b79c-4aecf7171d2a/adma202418219-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/210ae635-9f42-4910-b79c-4aecf7171d2a/adma202418219-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2d03793d-7469-41ac-8093-5d73cc826aed/adma202418219-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/210ae635-9f42-4910-b79c-4aecf7171d2a/adma202418219-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202418219-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202418219" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The Na-storage mechanism of the HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode material. a) The 2D contour plot of in situ XRD and the corresponding GCD curves during the second cycle. b) Lattice parameter variations during the first two cycles. c) Comparison of volume variation (ΔV/V<sub>pristine</sub>) with some reported NASICON cathodes of SIBs. d) Schematic structural change during charge/discharge processes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To analyze the localized Na<sup>+</sup> migration characteristic and evaluate the effects of five TMs on different Na sites, Density functional theory (DFT) calculations was performed on HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode material. The <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202418219-fig-0004">4</a> summarize the various Na<sup>+</sup> (near the different TM combinations) migration pathways and the corresponding migration energy barriers. Considering the inactivity of Na1 sites,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0007_R_d13056323e1191" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Energy. Lett. Chin. Chem. Lett.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> three kinds of possible migration pathways are reasonably constructed: Na1→Na2, Na2→Na1 and Na2→Na2. It is worth noting that the Na<sup>+</sup> environment in the high-entropy material is diverse, and when calculating the Na2 to Na2 sites, we directionally label the migration pathway as Na2→Na2'. In order to fully analyze the energy barriers of Na<sup>+</sup> under the same migration path in environment of different TMs combinations, the specific migration paths were divided into fourteen combinations based on the Mn, V, Fe, Cr, and Ti elements. According to the comparison of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0004">4a,b</a>, the overall energy barrier between the Na1 and Na2 is significantly lower than that of Na2→Na2', indicating that the Na1 site in HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP may be partially activated.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0019_R_d13056323e1206" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0004">4a</a>, for the migration between Na1 and Na2 sites, the energy barriers of Na2→Na1 is significantly lower than Na1→Na2, except that the combination of Cr-Cr, indicating that Na2→Na1 is the most favorable pathway, which is consistent with previous report.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0003_R_d13056323e1214" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater. Carbon Neutralization Int. J. Miner. Metall. Mater. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. Chem. Eng. J.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, four combinations with excellent migration ability under all three pathways are selected and shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0004">4c</a>. And their migration energy barriers under three pathways are basically below 1 eV. All five TMs&nbsp;are involved in the optimal combinations, which also further illustrates the excellent Na-storage performance of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP materials. In addition, according to the value of migration energy barriers, the other combinations are categorized as: migration barriers higher only between Na2→Na2, only exist the migration between Na2→Na2 and migration energy barrier higher than 1 eV under three pathways (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12–S14</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202418219-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d59bc1bc-1733-4d9a-a694-4a74de8574a5/adma202418219-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d59bc1bc-1733-4d9a-a694-4a74de8574a5/adma202418219-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/56f78b3b-58ac-4821-b699-7934b95b9390/adma202418219-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d59bc1bc-1733-4d9a-a694-4a74de8574a5/adma202418219-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202418219-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202418219" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">DFT calculated Na<sup>+</sup> migration pathways and the corresponding migration energy barriers under the different migration paths and TMs combinations. a) Na1→Na2 and Na2→Na1. b) Na2→Na2'. c) The optimal combinations of Mn-Ti, Mn-V, V-Cr, and Cr-Fe.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Furthermore, the electrochemical properties of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP at deep de/sodiation and wide operation temperature were further studied by GCD and CV tests. Compared with the GCD curve of 2–4.45 V, the HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP at 1.5–4.45 V shows a distinct voltage plateau below 2 V, as shown in <b>Figures</b> <a href="#adma202418219-fig-0005">5a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information), which belongs to V<sup>2+/3+</sup> redox, well corresponding to the CV results. The HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode also exhibits remarkable rate performance and cycling stability. The discharge specific capacity is 148.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5C, 93.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 15C and 77.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at 30C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). After 800 cycles at 5C, the capacity attenuation of each cycle is only 0.026%. The HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode material also demonstrates outstanding wide temperature range performance. The GCD curves at 25, 0, and −25&nbsp;°C, and the discharge specific capacities decrease slightly, which is ≈147.2 and 125.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0 and −25&nbsp;°C, respectively. Although the discharge specific capacity is different, these GCD curves intersect at the voltage of ≈3.25 V, indicating that the high-voltage redox is more complete when the temperature is lowered. It is noteworthy that the voltage plateau located at 4.0 V is more prominent at low temperatures, and this phenomenon is also appeared in Na<sub>4</sub>MnCr(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0020_R_d13056323e1292" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, to investigate the redox reactions in the high-voltage range at different operation temperatures, the ex-situ XPS was performed (inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0005">5a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information)). The Mn<sup>2+/3+/4+</sup>, V<sup>2+/3+4+/5+</sup>, Fe<sup>2+/3+/4+</sup>, and Ti<sup>3+/4+</sup> redox couples are involved in charge compensation during the GCD process. The fitted XPS spectra indicates the Mn<sup>3+/4+</sup> and V<sup>4+/5+</sup> redox reactions at high-voltage plateau of above 4.0 V, and these reactions are more thorough when the test temperature was lowered. Specifically, the high voltage conversions of Mn<sup>4+/3+</sup> and V<sup>5+/4+</sup> at −25&nbsp;°C are 52.4% and 61.4%, respectively, much higher than that of the 45.3% and 56.7% at 25&nbsp;°C. In addition, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0005">5b</a> illustrates the discharge specific capacity and the corresponding proportion in the different voltage region (H, M, and L regions) at different temperatures of 25, 0, and −25&nbsp;°C. As expected, −25&nbsp;°C has the highest the discharge specific capacity proportion in H and M regions, which indicates that reducing the operation temperature is conducive to the conversion of Mn<sup>4+/2+</sup> and V<sup>5+/3+</sup>. The CV curves and the polarization of the redox peaks at different temperatures are displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information). It is clear that the redox peaks are still well-defined even at −25&nbsp;°C, and the voltage polarizations are moderate, which further proves the excellent low-temperature performance of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202418219-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/65ad391b-8542-4953-8c2d-67127c7ebbe7/adma202418219-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/65ad391b-8542-4953-8c2d-67127c7ebbe7/adma202418219-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bb340614-38df-49da-ade8-f3103c1669ca/adma202418219-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/65ad391b-8542-4953-8c2d-67127c7ebbe7/adma202418219-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202418219-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202418219" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical and kinetic properties of the HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode material (1.5-4.45 V) in wide-temperature-range. a) Typical GCD profiles at −25, 0, and 25&nbsp;°C (insets: the high voltage conversion rate of Mn and V and enlarged GCD curves). b) Discharge specific capacity values and proportions of different voltage intervals calculated from the discharge curves in (a). c) Rate performance at −25, 0, and 25&nbsp;°C. d) Cycling performance at −25, 0, and 25&nbsp;°C. e) The dissolved rate of five TMs in electrolyte at −25 and 25&nbsp;°C. f) Rate and cycling performance under extremely cold conditions (−40 and 60&nbsp;°C). g) GITT curves at −25, 0, and 25&nbsp;°C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Moreover, the rate and cycling performance of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode at different operation temperatures were evaluated by GCD tests, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0005">5c,d</a>. When the operation temperature drops at 0&nbsp;°C, the discharge specific capacity is 83.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 15C and 68.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at 30C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19a</a>, Supporting Information). At −25&nbsp;°C, the discharge specific capacities can even reach 63.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at the high rate of 15C, the capacity retention is more than 50% compared with the 0.1C, and the corresponding GCD curves are displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19b</a> (Supporting Information). The cycling performance further illustrate the improved stability at lower operation temperature. Specifically, after 500 cycles at 5C, the capacity retention at 0 and −25&nbsp;°C is as high as 96.4% and 91.8%, which is more excellent than that of 85.7% at 25&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0005">5d</a>). The main reason for the superior low-temperature electrochemical performance of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP is that the dissolution of TM ions is inhibited at the low temperature,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0021_R_d13056323e1382" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Commun.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which also be verified by the inductively coupled plasma-mass spectrometry (ICP-MS) results. With the operation temperature of 25 and −25&nbsp;°C as examples, the dissolution rate of five TMs after 100 cycles was tested, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0005">5e</a>, the dissolution rate of TMs at −25&nbsp;°C is much less than at 25&nbsp;°C,&nbsp;indicating that low temperature can inhibit the dissolution of TMs. The cold environment can significantly inhibit the migration of TM ions from crystal to the electrolyte by pinning to their original position, thus hindering TMs redox loss and crystal lattice shrinkage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0006_R_d13056323e1391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces. Adv. Mater. Sci. China. Mater.">6</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0021_R_d13056323e1391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Commun.">21</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0022_R_d13056323e1391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The dissolution order from highest to lowest is Fe, V, Mn, Cr, and Ti, which can also be used to guide the design of subsequent NASICON materials. Furthermore, the Na-storage performance of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP at wide temperature range was evaluated at high-temperature of 60&nbsp;°C and extreme low-temperature of −50&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0005">5f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> (Supporting Information)). Specifically, the discharge specific capacities of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode at 60&nbsp;°C can even reach 150.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5C and achieve the capacity retention at 5C is 82.9% after 100 cycles. At −40&nbsp;°C, the discharge specific capacity is 108.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1C, and the capacity attenuation of each cycle is only 0.018% after 400 cycles at 0.5C. Most importantly, even at −50&nbsp;°C, it can deliver the specific capacity of 73.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1C, and the capacity retention can reach 92.8% after 90 cycles, which further proves the excellent electrochemical performance of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP at extremely cold conditions, broadening the application range of the NASICON cathode for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0023_R_d13056323e1416" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nat. Commun. Adv. Energy. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The comparison of specific capacity, cycle number, capacity retention and low temperature performance with other high-entropy NASICON cathodes has been listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode exhibits satisfactory electrochemical performance, especially at low temperature. Subsequently, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics at different operation temperature were investigated by GITT tests to certify the superior wide temperature range electrochemical performance of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0005">5g</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a> (Supporting Information)). Over the whole temperature of 25, 0, and −25&nbsp;°C, the <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode is 10<sup>−9</sup>–10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>. Specifically, the <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> at 25&nbsp;°C display small fluctuation and the diffusion coefficient curves is steady and has no obvious twist. Although the values of <i>D</i><sub>app,Na</sub> has a significantly reduced in the plateau area at −25&nbsp;°C, it is even comparable to that of 0&nbsp;°C. All the results prove that HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP has excellent Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, which explains the excellent electrochemical performance at low temperatures.</p>
         
         <p>The evaluation of full-cell is integral to prove the potency of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP in large-scale applications. As a common anode material of SIBs, commercial hard carbon (HC) was selected to assemble the full-cell (HC//HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a> (Supporting Information) shows the XRD curve and SEM image of HC. And the reversible capacity of HC is ≈329 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current density of 13 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a> (Supporting Information), and the appropriate rate performance also indicate that HC is a suitable anode material. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202418219-fig-0006">6a</a> shows the working principle of the full-cell, and Na<sup>+</sup> de-intercalates from the HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode and migrates toward the HC anode during charging process, and the discharge process is opposite. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0006">6b</a> shows the normalized GCD curves of HC anode, HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode, and HC//HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP full-cell, and the voltage plateau of HC//HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP full-cell is slightly lower than that of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0006">6c</a>, the GCD curves of HC//HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP at 0.1C coincide well, indicating that it has excellent reversibility. In addition, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0006">6d</a>, the redox peak profile of the HC//HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP full-cell is consistent with the HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0006">6e</a> enumerates in detail the rate performance of the HC//HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP, which deliveries the discharge capacities of 156.8, 146.5, 124.3, 102.5, and 83.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1C, 0.2C, 1C, 5C, and 15C, respectively, and the corresponding GCD curves are exhibited in the inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0006">6e</a>, even at 15C, the obvious charging and discharging plateaus are retained, indicating the excellent rate capability. As for the cycling performance, the HC//HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP full-cell still possesses the capacity retention of more than 80% after 250 cycles at 1C, and the inset displays the GCD curves of different cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0006">6f</a>). In addition, the HC//HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP full-cell can light the LEDs (inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0006">6f</a>), which also demonstrates the promise of practical applications of as-designed HC//HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP full-cell. Furthermore, the assembled HC//HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP full-cell can deliver the specific capacity of 103.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> based on the total active mass of cathode and anode, and the corresponding energy density is ≈301 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0006">6g</a>. The extensive data (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202418219-fig-0006">6g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a> (Supporting Information)) shows that HC//HE- Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP has outstanding advantage in terms of capacity, voltage, energy density, cycle number and capacity retention compared to the state-of-the-art reported full-cell configurations in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0003_R_d13056323e1557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater. Carbon Neutralization Int. J. Miner. Metall. Mater. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. Chem. Eng. J.">3</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0005_R_d13056323e1557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy. Environ. Mater. Acta Phys.-Chim. Sin.">5</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0007_R_d13056323e1557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Energy. Lett. Chin. Chem. Lett.">7</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0014_R_d13056323e1557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano Energy Energy. Storage. Mater. Adv. Energy. Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0016_R_d13056323e1557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Small">16</a>, <a href="#adma202418219-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0024_R_d13056323e1557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Small Methods Small New J. Chem.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202418219-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0e8c2c47-29fe-42d2-9004-51ed000ddb4c/adma202418219-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0e8c2c47-29fe-42d2-9004-51ed000ddb4c/adma202418219-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a27fc253-4b3b-46c5-8cf5-4a155f0f4e56/adma202418219-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0e8c2c47-29fe-42d2-9004-51ed000ddb4c/adma202418219-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202418219-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202418219" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the HC//HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP full-cell. a) Schematic illustration. b) The GCD curves of the HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP cathode, HC anode, and HC//HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP full-cell. c) The 3rd-5th GCD curves at 0.1C. d) The first five CV curves at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. e) Rate performance and the corresponding GCD curves from 0.1C to 15C. f) Cycling performance at 1C (insets: the GCD curves of different cycles and the digital photo of LEDs enlightened by HC//HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP full-cell). g) Comparison of capacity, voltage, and energy density between this work and other reported Na-ion full cells.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adma202418219-bib-0003" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202418219-bib-0003_R_d13056323e1592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater. Nano Energy Energy. Storage. Mater. Adv. Energy.                </figcaption>

         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202418219-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202418219-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, the single phase high-entropy NASICON-type phosphate cathode material HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP was prepared by ultrafast HTS technique. Benefiting from the high-entropy effect, the HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP achieves the reversible and stable multielectron redox reaction (2.4/2.8 e<sup>−</sup> at voltage range of 2.0–4.45/1.5–4.45 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), fabulous cycling stability (83.7% capacity retention after 2000 cycles at 5C) and excellent wide-temperature range operation from −50 to 60&nbsp;°C. In addition, in-situ XRD and ex-situ XPS tests reveal the charge compensation effect and the sequential redox process during Na<sup>+</sup> de-/intercalation reaction, illustrating the approximate solid solution phase transition behavior of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP. The DFT calculations were performed to analyze the localized Na<sup>+</sup> migration characteristic and evaluate the effects of Mn, V, Fe, Cr, and Ti on different Na sites. The result demonstrates that HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP has ideal migration pathways and energy barriers, which further confirm the superior reaction kinetics. The HC//HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP full-cell delivers a specific capacity of 103.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and an energy density of ≈301 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> based on the total active mass of cathode and anode, validating the application feasibility of HE-Na<sub>3.45</sub>TMP. This work combines multi-TM ions regulation and ultrafast synthesis to promote the formation of approximate solid solution phase transition, which provides a broad platform for the development of polyanionic materials for long-lifespan and all-climate SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
